Definition and Rulings of Hady: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Value Substitution
Definition and intra-Hanafi dispute on vow fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Hady, identify its permissible types, and explain the three Hanafi transmissions regarding whether value can substitute for a vowed sacrificial animal.

Core Idea
A vow to offer Hady obligates the animal's slaughter; its value alone is insufficient in the preferred Hanafi view.

This passage begins the chapter on Hady (sacrificial animals sent to the Haram). It first defines Hady linguistically and legally, noting that it must be from camels, cows, or sheep/goats. The minimum is a sheep, the best a camel. The main focus then shifts to the rulings of vows (nadhr) concerning Hady. A key dispute among Hanafi imams is whether a person who vows to offer Hady can fulfill that vow by giving the monetary value instead of the animal. The passage presents three transmissions and determines which is the relied-upon madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Define Hady and identify its types
Read the opening of the passage to understand that Hady is an animal sent to the Haram, specifically camels, cows, and sheep. Note that the minimum is a sheep, the best is a camel, and cows are intermediate.
This sets the scope of what qualifies as Hady.
2
Understand how an animal becomes Hady
The passage explains that an animal becomes Hady by explicit declaration or by implication, such as driving a marked animal to Mecca, even without verbal intention, because custom indicates it is for sacrifice.
Intention and customary practice play a role.
3
Identify the three riwayat on value substitution
When a person vows to offer Hady and specifies an animal whose blood is poured, there are three transmissions: Abu Suleiman allows value, Abu Hafs requires the exact animal, Ibn Sam'ah requires the animal but allows sending value to purchase and slaughter in Mecca.
Each is based on a different analogy.
4
Analyze the tarjih evidence
The passage cites the case of vowing two sheep and offering one sheep worth two: this is not acceptable, which supports Ibn Sam'ah's view. Also, Al-Hakim's interpretation suggests that even Abu Suleiman's narration allows value only if used to buy and slaughter in Mecca.
The case of two sheep is a decisive proof for the madhhab.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The text states that Ibn Sam'ah's narration is the madhhab: value alone is not sufficient; the animal must be slaughtered, but one can send value to purchase and slaughter on site.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Chapter of Hady. Its minimum is a sheep, and it includes camels, cows, and sheep. And whatever is permissible for Adahi (sacrificial animals for Eid) is permissible for hadaya (sacrificial animals). The sheep is permissible by istihsan. Lexically, it is what is sent to the Haram from a sheep, cow, or camel; one [animal] is called hadiya, just as it is said jadhi (young goat) in jidhiyya (a kind of saddle?). And it is said hadi with tashdid on the pattern of fa'il, and one [animal] is hadiya like matiyya, muti, mataya. Thus in al-Mughrib. His statement: Its minimum is a sheep, and it includes camels, cows, and sheep. This indicates that it has a maximum, and so it is: the best is camel, the minimum is sheep, cow is intermediate. Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both) interpreted 'what is easy of the Hady' as sheep. And he meant by camels, cows, and sheep, explanation of the types of what is sent to the Haram. So Hady, lexically and legally, is one [category], not that those types are called Hady without being sent to the Haram. Then, on that basis, the application of Hady to other than the three types in the speech of jurists in chapters of oaths and vows is metaphorical. Then, a single animal from livestock becomes Hady either by explicit declaration as Hady or by implication, which is either by intention or by driving a badana (camel/cow) to Mecca even without intention, because the intention of Hady is established customarily, for driving a badana to Mecca customarily is for Hady, not for riding or trade. Thus in al-Muhit. And he meant the driving after taqlid (marking), not mere driving. And he indicated by explaining the minimum that if one says: 'Upon me is to offer Hady' with no intention, then a sheep is binding upon him because it is the least. If he specifies something, it is binding. If it is something whose blood is poured (i.e., an animal that can be sacrificed), then there are three transmissions: In the narration of Abu Suleiman, it is permissible to offer its value because the obligation of the slave is considered by the obligation of Allah, and what Allah made obligatory in penalty of hunting can be fulfilled by value, so similarly what the slave obligates. In the narration of Abu Hafs, he is satisfied to offer its like (same animal) because it is in its meaning. In the narration of Ibn Sam'ah, it is not permissible to offer its value because he obligated two things: shedding of blood and charity, so it is not permissible to confine to charity, as in the Hady of tamattu' and qiran, unlike the penalty of hunting because it, as it obligated Hady, obligated something else, namely feeding. Here the vower obligated only Hady, so [the animal] is determined. And if he sends its value and buys its like in Mecca and slaughters it, it is permissible. Al-Hakim said in al-Mukhtasar: It is possible that this is an interpretation of Abu Suleiman's narration. And if one vows a sheep and offers a camel, he has done well, and this is not from value because shedding of blood is established in the higher substitute as in the original. They said: If he says 'Upon me to offer two sheep' and he offers one sheep worth two in value, it is not acceptable. And this gives weight to Ibn Sam'ah's narration, so it is the madhhab. And if the vowed item is not something whose blood is poured, then if it is... Chapter of Hady he offers its like, and then not. His statement: (And in Ibn Sam'ah's narration, it is not permissible to offer its value, its apparent meaning is that it is permissible to... there is a difference between it and Abu Hafs's narration, but the apparent wording of al-Nahr is that it is not permissible to offer its like either. Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on Hady (sacrificial animals sent to the Haram). It first defines Hady linguistically and legally, noting that it must be from the three types: camel, cow, sheep/goat. The minimum is a sheep, and the best is a camel. The text then discusses how an animal becomes Hady: by explicit declaration or by implication (e.g., driving a marked animal to Mecca). The main focus is on the rulings of vows (nadhr) regarding Hady. If someone vows to offer Hady without specifying an animal, the minimum (sheep) is binding. If he specifies, he must offer that type. However, there is a famous ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams on whether the value can be given in place of the animal. Three riwayat are presented: Abu Suleiman (value allowed), Abu Hafs (exact animal required), and Ibn Sam'ah (value not allowed, but can send value to buy and slaughter in Mecca). The preferred view (madhhab) is Ibn Sam'ah's, as supported by the case of vowing two sheep and offering one of equal value. The passage cuts off during discussion of non-blood items.

Key Terms
هدي (hady): An animal (camel, cow, sheep) sent to the Haram for sacrifice as part of Hajj or a vow.
تراق دمه (yaraqu damuhu): An animal whose blood is shed (i.e., eligible for sacrifice).
نذر (nadhr): A vow to make something obligatory upon oneself for the sake of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is acceptable because the value covers the obligation.
B) It is not acceptable; he must offer two separate sheep.
C) It is acceptable only if the sheep is slaughtered in the Haram.
D) It is acceptable only for vows, not for obligatory Hady.

Key Takeaway
When vowing Hady, the Hanafi madhhab requires the animal to be slaughtered; value alone is insufficient, but value can be sent to purchase and slaughter on site.

Definition and Minimum of Hady
Defining hady linguistically and legally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hady linguistically and legally, state the minimum and best types of animals, and explain how an animal becomes designated as hady.

Core Idea
Hady is only from camels, cows, or sheep; minimum is a sheep, and custom can establish the intention.

Hady is a key sacrificial offering in Hajj. This lesson clarifies what animals qualify, the minimum and best types, and how an animal attains the legal status of hady. Understanding these definitions ensures the ritual is performed correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its scope
The passage defines 'hady' linguistically as 'what is offered to the Haram' and legally as an animal from camels, cows, or sheep. Note that it must be offered to the Haram.
Hady is not any animal; only three types.
2
State the minimum and best
The minimum is a sheep (شاة). The best is a camel (بدنة). Cows are intermediate. This hierarchy matters for choosing the offering.
The author quotes Ibn Abbas interpreting 'what is easy from hady' as a sheep.
3
Differentiate linguistic and legal definitions
Linguistically, hady means any gift. Legally, it requires sending the specific animals to the Haram. Applying 'hady' to other animals in oaths is metaphorical.
This distinction prevents confusion in juristic usage.
4
Learn how an animal becomes hady
An animal becomes hady by: explicit statement, intention, or custom (driving it to Mecca). Custom suffices even without explicit intention because driving to Mecca is known for hady.
This is based on istihsan and custom (urf).
5
Apply to similar cases
The principle extends to any animal driven to Mecca with the customary intent of hady. This ruling is from the Hanafi school.
Compare with udhiyah (sacrifice for Eid) which has similar animal types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الهدى
أدناه شاة وهو إبل وبقر وغنم وما جاز في الضحايا جاز في الهدايا والشاة تجوز
باب الهدي
استحساناً
هو في اللغة ما يهدى إلى الحرم من شاة أو بقرة أو بعير الواحد هدية كما يقال جدي في جدية السرج. ويقال هدي بالتشديد على فعيل الواحدة هدية كمطية ومطي ومطايا. كذا في المغرب قوله : أدناه شاة وهو إبل وبقر وغنم يفيد أن له أعلى وهو كذلك فإنه الأفضل الإبل والأدنى الشاة والبقر وسط . وقد فسر ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما ما استيسر من الهدي بالشاة، وأراد بالإبل والبقر والغنم بيان أنواع ما يهدى إلى الحرم، فالهدي لغة وشرعاً واحد لا أن تلك الأنواع تسمى هدياً من غير إهداء إلى الحرم، وحنيئذ فإطلاق الهدي على غير الأنواع الثلاثة في كلام الفقهاء في باب الأيمان والنذور مجاز. ثم الواحد من النعم يكون هدياً بجعله صريحاً هدياً أو دلالة، وهي إما بالنية أو بسوق بدنة إلى مكة وإن لم ينو لأن نية الهدي ثابتة عرفاً لأن سوق البدنة إلى مكة في العرف يكون للهدي لا للركوب والتجارة. كذا في المحيط.
Chapter of Hady. The minimum of it is a sheep, and it includes camels, cows, and sheep. And whatever is permissible for udhiyah is permissible for hady, and the sheep is permissible by istihsan. It is linguistically what is offered to the Haram from a sheep, cow, or camel; the singular is hadiyah. It is also said hadi with tashdid on the pattern of fa'il, the singular is hadiyah like matiyyah, mati, and mataya. Thus in al-Mughrib. His saying: Its minimum is a sheep, and it is camels, cows, and sheep indicates that it has a maximum, and that is so: the best is camels, the minimum is sheep, and cows are intermediate. Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) interpreted 'what is easy from hady' as a sheep. He meant by camels, cows, and sheep to explain the types of what is offered to the Haram. So hady is one thing linguistically and legally, not that these types are called hady without being offered to the Haram. Then, when hady is applied to other than the three types in the speech of jurists in the chapters of oaths and vows, it is metaphorical. Then a single animal becomes hady by making it explicitly hady or implicitly, which is either by intention or by driving a camel to Mecca even if he did not intend, because the intention of hady is established by custom, since driving a camel to Mecca is customarily for hady, not for riding or trade. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling that clarifies the legal term 'hady' (sacrificial animal for Hajj). The author begins by stating the minimum hady is a sheep, and explains that hady includes camels, cows, and sheep. He then elaborates on the linguistic and legal definitions, noting that the best type is a camel, the minimum a sheep, and cows are intermediate. The passage also addresses how an animal becomes hady: either by explicit designation or implicitly through intention or by driving the animal to Mecca, since custom establishes the intention for hady. This ruling is based on istihsan and follows the interpretation of Ibn Abbas.

Key Terms
هدي (hady): An animal offered to the Haram (Mecca) for Hajj, specifically a camel, cow, or sheep.
شاة (shah): A sheep, the minimum acceptable for hady.
بدنة (badanah): A camel (or sometimes a cow) offered as hady; the best type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hady must be a camel, cow, or sheep offered to the Haram; the minimum is a sheep, and custom can establish the intention for hady.

Vows Regarding Hady: Three Transmissions
Rulings on a vow to offer hady, including three different transmissions within the Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three transmissions regarding the fulfillment of a hady vow and apply the Hanafi relied-upon position that only the animal itself suffices.

Core Idea
A vow to offer hady requires the animal itself; paying the value is insufficient according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

This passage explores an intra-Hanafi dispute on a specific type of vow: vowing to offer a hady (sacrificial animal). The core question is whether the vower can fulfill the vow by paying the monetary value instead of slaughtering the animal. The text presents three transmissions from early Hanafi masters, and then settles on the position that only the animal itself suffices. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the Hanafi school resolves internal differences through tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and basic ruling
What is the vow? The minimum is a sheep if unspecified. If specified, that animal is required.
2
Examine the three transmissions
What does each of Abu Sulayman, Abu Hafs, and Ibn Sama'a say about substituting value?
Abu Sulayman: value allowed. Abu Hafs: similar animal required. Ibn Sama'a: only the exact animal.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
Why is Ibn Sama'a's view preferred? The vower obliged both blood spilling and charity; value fails to achieve the blood spilling.
Unlike hunting penalty where both hady and feeding are combined.
4
Apply the ruling to related cases
How does the ruling apply to vowed items that are not animals (movable, immovable)? What if the wording includes an invalidating destination?
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: the vower must slaughter an animal; paying value alone is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد ببيان الأدنى أنه لو قال الله على أن أهدي ولا نية له فإنه يلزمه شاة لأنها الأقل، وإن عين شيئاً لزمه، فإن كان مما يراق دمه ففيه ثلاث روايات في رواية أبي سليمان يجوز أن يهدي بقيمته لأن إيجاب العبد معتبر بإيجاب الله تعالى وما أوجبه الله تعالى في جزاء الصيد يتأدى بالقيمة فكذا ما أوجبه العبد، وفي رواية أبي حفص أجزأه أن يهدي مثله لأنه في معناه، وفي رواية ابن سماعة لا يجوز أن يهدي قيمته لأنه أوجب شيئين: الإراقة والتصدق فلا يجوز الاقتصار على التصدق كما في هدي المتعة والقرآن بخلاف جزاء الصيد لأنه كما أوجب الهدي أوجب غيره وهو الإطعام، وهنا الناذر ما أوجب إلا الهدي فتعين ولو بعث بقيمته فاشترى بمكة مثله وذبحه جاز. قال الحاكم في المختصر ويحتمل أن يكون هذا تأويل رواية أبي سليمان. ومن نذر شاة فأهدى جزوراً فقد أحسن وليس هذا من القيمة لثبوت الإراقة في البدل الأعلى كالأصل وقالوا : إذا قال الله علي أن أهدي شاتين فأهدي شاة تساوي شاتين قيمة لم يجزه وهي مرجحة لرواية ابن سماعة فكان هو المذهب. وإن كان المنذور شيئاً لا يراق دمه فإن كان منقولاً تصدق بعينه أو بقيمته وإن كان عقاراً تصدق بقيمته ولا يتعين التصدق به في الحرم ولا على فقراء مكة لأن الهدي فيه مجاز عن التصدق. ثم اعلم أنه إذا ألحق بلفظ الهدي ما يبطله لا يلزمه شيء كما لو قال هذه الشاة هدي إلى الحرم أو إلى المسجد الحرام عند أبي حنيفة لأن اسم الهدي إنما يوجب باعتبار اضمار مكة بدلالة العرف فإذا صرح بالحرم أو المسجد تعذر هذا الإضمار إذ قد صرح بمراده .
And he indicated by explaining the minimum that if someone says 'I owe it to Allah to offer a hady' without any intention, then a sheep is incumbent upon him because it is the least. And if he specifies something, that is incumbent. If it is something whose blood is to be spilled, then there are three transmissions: In the transmission of Abu Sulayman, it is permissible for him to offer its value because the obligation of a servant is considered by the obligation of Allah, and what Allah obliged in the penalty for hunting is fulfilled by value, so similarly what the servant obliged. In the transmission of Abu Hafs, it suffices him to offer its like because it is similar in meaning. In the transmission of Ibn Sama'a, it is not permissible for him to offer its value because he obliged two things: the spilling of blood and charity, so it is not permissible to restrict to charity, as in the hady of tamattu' and qiran, unlike the penalty for hunting because there he obliged both the hady and something else which is feeding, and here the vower obliged only the hady, so it is specified. And if he sends its value and buys its like in Mecca and slaughters it, it is permissible. Al-Hakim said in al-Mukhtasar: it is possible that this is the interpretation of Abu Sulayman's transmission. And whoever vows a sheep and offers a camel has done well, and this is not from the value because the spilling of blood is established in the superior substitute as in the original. And they said: if someone says 'I owe Allah to offer two sheep' and he offers one sheep equal in value to the two, it is not permissible, and this weighs in favor of Ibn Sama'a's transmission, so that is the madhhab. And if the vowed thing is something whose blood is not spilled, then if it is movable, he gives charity with the object itself or its value; if it is immovable, he gives charity with its value, and it is not specified that the charity be in the Haram or to the poor of Mecca, because hady here is metaphorical for charity. Then know that if he attaches to the word hady something that invalidates it, nothing is incumbent upon him, as if he says 'this sheep is hady to the Haram' or 'to the Sacred Mosque' according to Abu Hanifa, because the name hady only obligates by virtue of assuming Mecca by custom; when he explicitly states the Haram or the Mosque, this assumption becomes impossible since he has stated his intent.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a vow to offer hady. The author begins with the case where someone vows 'I owe Allah to offer a hady' without specifying the animal. According to the text, a sheep is required because it is the minimum. If he specifies a particular animal, that must be given. For animals whose blood is to be spilled (i.e., cattle), there are three transmissions: Abu Sulayman allows giving the value; Abu Hafs requires giving an animal similar in value; Ibn Sama'a strictly requires the animal itself, because the vow obligates both slaughtering and charity, and one cannot substitute only charity. The text states that Ibn Sama'a's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). For items that are not animals (e.g., food, property), the ruling is to give charity with the item or its value, and it is not restricted to Mecca. Finally, if the wording of the vow includes something that invalidates the hady concept (e.g., specifying 'to the Haram' instead of Mecca), then nothing is owed, because the legal effect of 'hady' depends on the implied destination of Mecca.

Key Terms
نذر (nathr): A vow to perform an act of worship; here specifically vowing to offer a hady.
رواية (riwayah): A transmission of a legal ruling within the Hanafi school, attributed to a specific narrator (Abu Sulayman, Abu Hafs, Ibn Sama'a).
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value; offering the value instead of the vowed animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in this passage, can a person fulfill a vow to offer a sheep for hady by paying its monetary value?
A) Yes, according to Abu Sulayman's transmission
B) Yes, according to Abu Hafs' transmission
C) No, according to Ibn Sama'a's transmission, which is preferred
D) No, if the sheep is specified

Key Takeaway
A vow to offer hady must be fulfilled by slaughtering the animal; paying its value is insufficient according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Hady and Udhiyah: Age, Sharing, and Defect Conditions
Rulings on age, sharing, and defects in hady by analogy with udhiyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the age, sharing, and defect conditions for a valid hady by applying the Hanafi rule that what is valid in udhiyah is valid in hady.

Core Idea
Hady mirrors udhiyah: same age, sharing, and defect rules apply.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands the principle that sacrificial requirements for Eid (udhiyah) also apply to the Hajj sacrifice (hady). The author clarifies age specifications, the permissibility and conditions of sharing a camel or cow, and the necessity of being free from defects. A key nuance is the intra-Hanafi dispute on the exact age of a jadha' (young sheep) acceptable for hady. By understanding this analogy, you will be able to determine the validity of a hady in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Analogical Principle
The passage opens with the maxim 'ما جاز في الضحايا جاز في الهدايا'. Recognize that this establishes a direct transfer of rulings from udhiyah to hady. All subsequent details are applications of this principle.
This principle is a form of qiyas jali (clear analogy) between two devotional blood sacrifices.
2
Analyze Age Requirements
The thani (two-year-old) is required for camels, cows, and sheep. For sheep, a jadha' is acceptable but with a dispute: 7 months per al-Mabsut, or 8 months with a large body per Ghayat al-Bayan. Determine validity based on the animal's age and size.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both views are transmitted, so a mufti may follow either.
3
Understand Sharing (Ishtirak)
Up to seven persons may share a camel or cow for hady, provided all intend devotion (qurbah). The best method is to purchase together from the start. If the animal was already designated for hady, partners cannot be added later, as that constitutes a sale of a religious obligation.
If one partner is a disbeliever or wants meat only, the entire sharing is invalid.
4
Apply the Defect Rule
The passage states that the animal must be free from defects, just as in udhiyah. This is a general condition: any defect that invalidates udhiyah also invalidates hady.
Common defects include blindness, lameness, or severe illness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما جاز في الضحايا جاز في الهدايا يعني فيجوز الثني من الإبل والبقر والغنم ولا يجوز الجذع إلا من الضأن لأنه قربة تعلقت بإراقة الدم كالأضحية فيتخصصان بمحل واحد والثني من الغنم ما تم له سنة، ومن البقر ما تم له سنتان، ومن الإبل ما تم له خمس. واختلف في الجذع من الضأن فجزم في المبسوط أنه ابن سبعة أشهر عند الفقهاء في اللغة. وفي غاية البيان إنه ما تم له ثمانية أشهر وشرط أن يكون عظيم الجثة، أما إن كان صغيراً فلا بد من تمام السنة. وأفاد أنه يجوز الإشتراك في بدنه كما في الأضحية بشرط إرادة الكل القربة وإن اختلفت أجناسهم من دم متعة وإحصار وجزاء صيد وغير ذلك، ولو كان الكل من جنس واحد كان أحب بأن اشتري بدنة لمتعة مثلاً ناوياً إن يشترك فيها ستة أو يشتريها بغير نية الهدي ثم يشترك فيه ستة ينووا الهدي أو يشتروها معاً الابتداء وهو الأفضل. وأما إذا اشتراها للهدي من غير نية الشركة ليس له الاشتراك فيها لأنه بيعاً لأنها كلها صارت واجبة بعضها بإيجاب الشرع وما زاد بإيجابه، وإذا كان أحد الشركاء كافراً أو مريداً اللحم دون الهدي لم يجزهم، وإذا مات أحد الشركاء فرضي وارثه أن ينحرها عن الميت معهم أجزأهم استحساناً لأن المقصود هو التصدق، وأي الشركاء نحرها النحر أجزأ الكل، وأشار إلى أنه لا بد من السلامة عن العيوب كما في الأضحية فهو يوم مطرد منعكس أي فما لا يجوز في الضحايا لا يجوز في الهدايا، فعبارة الهداية أولى وهي : يصير في اختلفت أجناسهم الخ هذا صريح في خلاف ما قدمه في القرآن والجنايات من أن الاشتراك لا يكفي في الجنايات بخلاف دم الشكر ونبهنا عليه هناك فلا تغفل، وما هنا اللباب أيضاً . وصح قوله (وأما إذا اشتراها للهدى من غير نية الشركة الخ) ذكر في أضحية الدرر لو أخذ إشراك ستة في بدنة مشرية لأضحية استحساناً، وفي القياس لا تجوز وهو قول زفر لأنه أعدها للقربة فلا يجوز بيعها. وجه الاستحسان أنه قد يجد بقرة سمينة ولا يجد الشريك وقت الشراء فمست الحاجة إلى هذا وندب كون الاشتراك قبل الشراء ليكون أبعد عن الخلاف وعن صورة الرجوع في القربة اهـ. فعلى ما هنا تقييد ما في الدرر بما إذا نوى الشركة عند الشراء تأمل قوله : (ليس له الاشتراك فيها) قال في الفتح : فإن فعل فعليه أن يتصدق بالثمن قوله : فهو مطرد منعكس) أو رد عليه ما مر
His saying: 'And whatever is permissible in udhiyah is permissible in hady' means that the thani (two-year-old) of camels, cows, and sheep is permissible, and the jadha' is not permissible except from sheep, because it is a devotional act connected to spilling blood like udhiyah, so they are specific to one place. The thani of sheep is one year old, of cows two years, of camels five years. There is a difference of opinion regarding the jadha' of sheep: in al-Mabsut it is determined as seven months according to the jurists in language; in Ghayat al-Bayan it is eight months with the condition that it has a large body; if it is small, then a full year is required. And he indicated that sharing in a camel is permissible as in udhiyah, with the condition that all intend devotion, even if their types differ (blood of tamattu', ihsar, penalty for hunting, etc.). If all are of one type, it is more liked. He may buy a camel for tamattu' intending that six share in it, or he buys it without intention of hady then six share intending hady, or they buy it together from the start—the last is best. But if he buys it for hady without intention of sharing, he cannot include partners because it becomes a sale, since the entire animal becomes obligatory—part by obligation of the Shari'ah and the rest by his own obligation. If one of the partners is a disbeliever or intends the meat without hady, it is not permissible for them. If one of them dies and his heir agrees to slaughter it for him with them, it suffices by istihsan because the purpose is charity. Whichever partner slaughters, it suffices for all. And he indicated that it must be free from defects as in udhiyah, so it is a general rule: what is not permissible in udhiyah is not permissible in hady. The expression in al-Hidayah is better: it becomes clear that the disagreement on types contradicts what was presented in the chapters of Qur'an and penalties—that sharing is not sufficient in penalties except for blood of gratitude—and we have pointed that out. And what is here is also the core. And the statement 'If he buys it for hady without intention of sharing... is correct.' In the chapter on udhiyah in al-Durar, if he takes six partners in a camel purchased for udhiyah, it is permissible by istihsan, but by qiyas it is not, which is the view of Zufar, because he dedicated it for devotion, so selling it is not permitted. The basis of istihsan is that he might find a fat cow and not find a partner at the time of purchase, so there is need. And it is recommended that the sharing be before purchase to be further from dispute and from the appearance of withdrawal from devotion. So according to what is here, it restricts what is in al-Durar to when he intends sharing at the time of purchase. Reflect on his saying: 'He cannot include partners in it.' In al-Fath he said: if he does so, he must give the price in charity. And his saying: 'So it is a general rule' was responded to with what has passed.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage expands on the principle that what is valid for udhiyah (sacrificial animal for Eid) is also valid for hady (sacrificial animal for Hajj). The author covers several points: (1) Age: only thani (two-year-old) animals are allowed for cattle, except for sheep where a jadha' (seven or eight months) may be acceptable according to different views. (2) Sharing: up to seven persons may share in a camel or cow for hady, provided all intend devotion; the best method is to purchase jointly from the start. (3) If the animal was already designated for hady, one cannot add partners later because it would be a sale of a religious obligation. (4) Conditions: no partner may be a disbeliever or seek meat only; if a partner dies, the heir may agree to slaughter on his behalf. (5) Defects: the animal must be free of defects, analogous to udhiyah. The passage also notes a dispute regarding the permissibility of sharing in penalties for hunting compared to hady, but the rule for hady is that sharing is allowed as in udhiyah.

Key Terms
ثني (thani): A two-year-old animal; for sheep: one year old, for cows: two years, for camels: five years.
جذع (jadha'): A young sheep that is less than a year old but has reached a certain age (seven or eight months) and is large.
اشتراك (ishtirak): Sharing a single animal (camel or cow) among multiple persons for hady.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All partners must be of the same type (e.g., all for tamattu').
B) All partners must intend devotion (qurbah).
C) The animal must be bought on the day of sacrifice.
D) Only one person can slaughter.

Key Takeaway
What is valid in udhiyah is valid in hady, including age, sharing, and freedom from defects.

Animals Valid for Hadi: Ages and Conditions
Defining valid Hadi animals and their ages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the age requirements for animals valid for Hadi and identify the conditions under which a jadha' sheep is permissible.

Core Idea
Valid Hadi animals mirror Udhiya: thani for camels, cows, sheep; only sheep allows jadha' under size and age conditions.

In this lesson, we examine the specific age and type requirements for animals used as Hadi during Hajj. The author begins by analogizing to Udhiya, then details the exact ages for each species, and finally addresses the exceptional case of the jadha' — a younger sheep that may suffice under certain conditions. Understanding these rules ensures the sacrificial offering meets its legal validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The author states that whatever is valid for Udhiya is valid for Hadi. Recognize this as an analogy (qiyas) between two acts of worship that involve bloodshed.
This establishes a default rule: the animal requirements are identical unless specified otherwise.
2
List the species and required ages for thani
For sheep, thani means one year old; for cows, two years; for camels, five years. These are the standard ages for a fully valid Hadi animal.
Thani is the term for an animal that has reached its second physical maturity.
3
Analyze the jadha' exception
Jadha' (young sheep) is permissible only from sheep, with a condition: it must be large-bodied. There is a difference of opinion on its exact age (seven vs. eight months), but both agree on the size condition. If the jadha' is small, a full year is required.
This exception is specific to sheep; other species have no such allowance.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a pilgrim offers a 6-month-old sheep that is large-bodied, is it valid? According to the mufta bihi, it must be at least seven months old, so it would not be valid. If it is seven months and large, it is valid.
Always check both age and size for jadha'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما جاز في الضحايا جاز في الهدايا يعني فيجوز الثني من الإبل والبقر والغنم ولا يجوز الجذع إلا من الضأن لأنه قربة تعلقت بإراقة الدم كالأضحية فيتخصصان بمحل واحد والثني من الغنم ما تم له سنة، ومن البقر ما تم له سنتان، ومن الإبل ما تم له خمس. واختلف في الجذع من الضأن فجزم في المبسوط أنه ابن سبعة أشهر عند الفقهاء في اللغة. وفي غاية البيان إنه ما تم له ثمانية أشهر وشرط أن يكون عظيم الجثة، أما إن كان صغيراً فلا بد من تمام السنة.
His statement: 'And whatever is permissible in udhiya is permissible in hadi' means that the thani (two-year-old) from camels, cows, and sheep is permissible, and the jadha' (young) is not permissible except from sheep, because it is a worship that involves the shedding of blood like udhiya, so they are specified with one locus. The thani from sheep is what has completed one year, from cows what has completed two years, and from camels what has completed five years. There is a difference of opinion regarding the jadha' from sheep: in al-Mabsut it is definitively stated that it is seven months old according to the jurists in language. In Ghayat al-Bayan it is what has completed eight months, with the condition that it be large-bodied; if it is small, then a full year is required.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement defining which animals are valid for Hadi. The author begins by stating the general rule that whatever is valid for Udhiya (sacrificial animal for Eid) is valid for Hadi (sacrificial animal for Hajj). He then specifies the ages: for sheep, thani means one year old; for cows, two years; for camels, five years. The jadha' (young) from sheep is an exception: it is permissible although it is younger, but there is a scholarly difference over its exact age (seven vs. eight months) and it must be large-bodied. If it is small, it does not count until a full year. This ruling is based on the principle of analogy (qiyas) with Udhiya, as both involve shedding blood as an act of worship.

Key Terms
هدي (hady): Sacrificial animal offered in the Haram during Hajj, either voluntary or obligatory (e.g., for tamattu', qiran, or penalty).
ثني (thani): An animal that has completed its second year for sheep, third year for cows, and sixth year for camels.
جذع (jadha'): A young sheep that has not reached one year but is permitted for Hadi if it is large-bodied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum age for a cow to be valid as Hadi (as thani)?
A) One year
B) Two years
C) Five years
D) Three years

Key Takeaway
The general rule for Hadi animals is the same as for Udhiya; thani ages are fixed, and jadha' is allowed only from sheep with the conditions of being at least seven months old and large-bodied.

Sharing in a Badana for Hadi: Permissibility and Conditions
Ruling on sharing a camel/cow among multiple persons for Hadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which multiple persons may share a camel or cow for Hadi and identify when sharing is invalid.

Core Idea
A camel or cow suffices for seven persons in Hadi, provided all intend worship.

This lesson examines the permissibility of sharing a single camel or cow among multiple individuals for the sacrificial offering (hadi) during Hajj. The Hanafi school allows this practice under specific conditions, even when the obligations differ (e.g., tamattu', penalty), and clarifies when sharing becomes invalid. The ruling is grounded in istihsan (juristic preference) and analogy to udhiya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sharing (ishtirak) in a badana for hadi. The ruling: permissible (ja'iz) and recommended (ahab) when done from the start.
2
List the essential conditions
All partners must intend qurbah (worship); the animal must be free of defects; sharing is limited to a maximum of seven; partnership must be established before any obligation is fixed.
3
Distinguish valid and invalid scenarios
Valid: partners with different obligations. Invalid: a partner who is a disbeliever or intends only meat. If purchased alone for hadi, cannot include others later.
4
Note the istihsan ruling
If a partner dies and heir agrees to include the deceased, it is valid by istihsan.
Istihsan overrides the general prohibition of selling wajib items.
5
Apply the udhiya analogy
Defects that invalidate udhiya also invalidate hadi.
The text states: 'whatever is not permissible in udhiya is not permissible in hadi.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد أنه يجوز الإشتراك في بدنه كما في الأضحية بشرط إرادة الكل القربة وإن اختلفت أجناسهم من دم متعة وإحصار وجزاء صيد وغير ذلك، ولو كان الكل من جنس واحد كان أحب بأن اشتري بدنة لمتعة مثلاً ناوياً إن يشترك فيها ستة أو يشتريها بغير نية الهدي ثم يشترك فيه ستة ينووا الهدي أو يشتروها معاً الابتداء وهو الأفضل. وأما إذا اشتراها للهدي من غير نية الشركة ليس له الاشتراك فيها لأنه بيعاً لأنها كلها صارت واجبة بعضها بإيجاب الشرع وما زاد بإيجابه، وإذا كان أحد الشركاء كافراً أو مريداً اللحم دون الهدي لم يجزهم، وإذا مات أحد الشركاء فرضي وارثه أن ينحرها عن الميت معهم أجزأهم استحساناً لأن المقصود هو التصدق، وأي الشركاء نحرها النحر أجزأ الكل، وأشار إلى أنه لا بد من السلامة عن العيوب كما في الأضحية فهو يوم مطرد منعكس أي فما لا يجوز في الضحايا لا يجوز في الهدايا.
And he benefited that partnership in a camel/cow is permissible as in udhiya, with the condition that all intend the act of worship, even if their types differ—from blood of tamattu', ihsar, penalty of game, and others. If all are of the same type, it is more beloved, for example, he buys a camel for tamattu' intending to share it among six, or he buys it without intention of hadi then six share in it intending hadi, or they buy it together from the beginning, which is best. As for if he buys it for hadi without intention of partnership, he does not have the right to share it, because it is a sale; since all of it became obligatory, part by the obligation of the law and the rest by his own obligation. If one of the partners is a disbeliever or intends the meat without hadi, it does not suffice them. If one of the partners dies and his heir agrees to slaughter it for the deceased with them, it suffices them by istihsan because the purpose is charity. Whichever of the partners performs the slaughter, it suffices them all. And he indicated that it must be free from defects as in udhiya, so it is inclusive and exclusive, i.e., whatever is not permissible in udhiya is not permissible in hadi.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on sharing a camel or cow (badana) among multiple persons for Hadi. The author explains that a camel/cow suffices for up to seven persons, even if their reasons for the sacrifice differ (e.g., tamattu', ihsar, penalty for game). The best method is to purchase the animal together from the start with the intention of sharing. If someone buys it individually for his own hadi, he cannot later include others because that would constitute a sale of what has become wajib. A disbeliever or someone who only wants meat invalidates the share for all. If a partner dies and his heir agrees to slaughter on his behalf, it is valid by istihsan (juristic preference) because the underlying purpose is charity. The animal must be free from physical defects, mirroring the rules of Udhiya.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak): Partnership in a sacrificial animal, where multiple persons share the reward by contributing to a camel or cow.
بدنة (badana): A camel or cow (and sometimes a sheep?) that is offered as a sacrifice in the Haram.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to allow the sharing despite the prohibition of selling wajib items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One partner being a disbeliever
B) One partner intending only the meat
C) Both A and B
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Sharing a camel or cow for Hadi is permissible for up to seven persons provided all intend worship, but cannot be done after the animal becomes wajib for one person.

Reciprocity of the Udhiya-Hadi Rule and Its Exceptions
Critical refinement of the reciprocity of the udhiya-hadi rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi rule 'what is valid in udhiya is valid in hadi' is inclusive but not exclusive, and identify the two exceptions where a badana is required instead of a sheep.

Core Idea
A rule may be inclusive (mutarid) without being exclusive (mun'akis): sheep covers most hadi cases, but severe violations demand a camel.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines a common principle in Hajj sacrifices. While the general rule states that any animal valid for udhiya (sacrificial offering) is also valid for hadi (penalty or votive offering), the commentator clarifies that this rule is not fully reciprocal. There are cases where the severity of the offense demands a more expensive animal. Understanding this nuance prevents incorrect application of the rule in Hajj penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its intended scope
The rule is: 'ما جاز في الضحايا جاز في الهدايا' (what is valid in udhiya is valid in hadi). Recognize that this is a broad principle covering most cases of obligatory blood offerings in Hajj.
The rule is initially stated as if it were fully reciprocal.
2
Recognize the claim of full reciprocity and the Hanafi rebuttal
Some scholars claim the rule is mutarid mun'akis (inclusive and exclusive). The commentator refutes this, arguing it is only mutarid (inclusive).
The rebuttal uses an analogy from sale and hire.
3
Understand the analogy from sale/hire
Just as what can be a price in a sale can also be a rental fee, but not vice versa (due to different benefits), similarly, what is valid in udhiya is valid in hadi, but hadi has additional valid animals (e.g., camel for severe cases) not valid in udhiya.
This analogy shows the rule is not exclusive.
4
Locate the two specific exceptions
The text explicitly states: 'الشاة تجوز في كل شيء إلا في طواف الركن جنباً ووطء بعد الوقوف' (the sheep suffices in everything except tawaf of the corner while junub and intercourse after standing). In these two cases, a badana (camel) is wajib.
These exceptions are due to the severity of the violation.
5
Grasp the rationale (ta'lil) based on severity
The reason for the exceptions: janabah (major impurity) is more severe, and intercourse after wuquf is the highest type of benefit; thus a more expensive sacrifice is required to show the difference between minor and major violations.
The same logic applies to a menstruating or postpartum woman performing tawaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعبارة الهداية أولى وهي : يصير في اختلقت أجناسهم الخ هذا صريح في خلاف ما قدمه في القرآن والجنايات من أن الاشتراك لا يكفي في الجنايات بخلاف دم الشكر ونبهنا عليه هناك فلا تغفل، وما هنا اللباب أيضاً . وصح قوله (وأما إذا اشتراها للهدى من غير نية الشركة الخ) ذكر في أضحية الدرر لو أخذ إشراك ستة في بدنة مشرية لأضحية استحساناً، وفي القياس لا تجوز وهو قول زفر لأنه أعدها للقربة فلا يجوز بيعها. وجه الاستحسان أنه قد يجد بقرة سمينة ولا يجد الشريك وقت الشراء فمست الحاجة إلى هذا وندب كون الاشتراك قبل الشراء ليكون أبعد عن الخلاف وعن صورة الرجوع في القربة اهـ. فعلى ما هنا تقييد ما في الدرر بما إذا نوى الشركة عند الشراء تأمل قوله : (ليس له الاشتراك فيها) قال في الفتح : فإن فعل فعليه أن يتصدق بالثمن قوله : فهو مطرد منعكس) أو رد عليه ما مر

So the phrasing of al-Hidayah is more appropriate, which is: 'It becomes... their types differ etc.' This is explicit contrary to what he presented earlier in the chapters of Quran and injuries, that partnership does not suffice in injuries contrary to the blood of thanks, and we alerted to it there, so do not be heedless. And what is here is also the core. And his statement is correct (As for if he buys it for hadi without intention of partnership etc.) He mentioned in Udhiyat al-Durar: if he takes six partners in a camel purchased for udhiya, it is permissible by istihsan, and by qiyas it is not permissible, and it is the view of Zufar because he prepared it for worship so it cannot be sold. The basis of istihsan is that he may find a fat cow and not find a partner at the time of purchase, so there is need for this, and partnership before purchase is recommended to be further from disagreement and from the image of retracting from worship. So according to what is here, it restricts what is in al-Durar to when he intends partnership at the time of purchase. Ponder his statement: 'He does not have the right to share in it.' He said in al-Fath: if he does so, he must give the price in charity. His statement: 'So it is inclusive and exclusive' or rebutted what passed.

In everything except for tawaf of the corner while junub and intercourse after standing, and he eats from voluntary hadi. And it is not permissible in hadi except what is permissible in udhiya, for the reciprocal is not necessitated from the inclusive. Do you not see to their statement: Whatever is valid as a price in sale is valid as a rental fee in hire? Its reciprocal does not necessarily hold because it is invalidated by the permissibility of different benefits as rental fees but not as prices. His statement: (The sheep suffices in everything except for tawaf of the corner while junub and intercourse after standing) means that every place in which blood is mentioned in the book of Hajj, the sheep suffices except in what he mentioned. And his intention is not generalization, for whoever vows a camel or a small camel, the sheep does not suffice him. Rather, the badana is necessary when he performs tawaf while junub because janabah is more severe, so it is obligatory to compensate its deficiency with a badana to show the difference between the minor and major. And analogized to it is if a woman performs tawaf while menstruating or postpartum, and there is no third place as in Fath al-Qadir, because the meaning that necessitates severity is one. And it is obligatory in intercourse after standing because it is the highest type of benefits, so its cause is severe. And he made it general to include after shaving, and we have already mentioned a difference of opinion regarding it: the sheep and the preponderant is that it is obligatory after it. So what is meant here is intercourse after standing before shaving and tawaf.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing the reciprocity of the rule 'What is valid in Udhiya is valid in Hadi.' The commentator argues that the rule is inclusive (every valid udhiya animal is valid for hadi) but not fully exclusive (there are things outside udhiya that are valid for hadi, e.g., a camel for severe violations). He gives the example of tawaf while in a state of major ritual impurity (junub) and intercourse after standing at Arafat, where a sheep is insufficient and a badana (camel) is required. The reason is that these acts have a greater severity, and the sacrifice must match the gravity. The discussion also includes a note on the dispute with Zufar regarding sharing in a badana after purchase. The overall lesson is that while the basic rule holds, it has exceptions based on the nature of the violation.

Key Terms
مطرد (mutarid): Inclusive: all cases of the subject (udhiya) apply to the object (hadi).
منعكس (mun'akis): Exclusive: the rule applies only to the subject and not beyond.
طواف الركن (tawaf al-rukun): The obligatory circumambulation of the Kaaba, also known as tawaf al-ifada.
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
بدنة (badana): A camel, specifically the sacrificial camel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which two scenarios does a sheep not suffice for the required sacrifice in Hajj?
A) Tawaf al-ifada and sa'i
B) Tawaf while junub and intercourse after standing at Arafat
C) Vow of a camel and shaving the head early
D) Ihram for tamattu' and qiran

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule is that a sheep suffices for most hadi obligations, but severe violations like tawaf while junub or intercourse after wuquf require a badana (camel) because of the greater severity.

Eating from the Hadi: Permissibility and Recommended Practice
Ruling with proof on eating from Hadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating from the Hadi, identify the types from which it is allowed, and explain the proof from the Prophet's action and analogy to Udhiya.

Core Idea
Eating from voluntary Hadi, tamattu', and qiran is recommended; from obligatory Hadi it is not allowed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common misconception about the Hadi sacrifice. Many assume the offerer cannot eat from it, but the Hanafi school permits and even recommends eating from specific types. The ruling is based on the Prophet's own practice in the Farewell Pilgrimage and analogical reasoning with the Eid sacrifice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Types of Hadi
Note the three types mentioned: voluntary (tatawwu'), tamattu', and qiran. These are categories from which eating is permitted.
The passage uses the phrase 'only' (faqat) to limit permission.
2
Determine the Ruling
The ruling is that eating is permissible and recommended (mustahab). The offerer may eat part, not all, as indicated by the word 'from' (min).
The recommended distribution is to divide into thirds: charity, gift, and keep.
3
Analyze the Proof
The proof is the Prophet's action in Hajjat al-Wada', where he ate from the meat of his sacrifice. This establishes the sunnah.
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim.
4
Apply the Analogy to Udhiya
The ruling is analogized to Udhiya (Eid sacrifice), where eating is also permissible. The same recommended distribution applies.
This analogy is based on the shared illa of being a ritual blood offering.
5
Note the Condition
The animal must have reached the Haram (sacred precincts) to be considered Hadi. If slaughtered before, the rules of Hadi do not apply.
This condition is implied by the term 'hady' which requires the animal to reach the Haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويأكل من هدى التطوع والمتعة والقرآن فقط أي يجوز له الأكل ويستحب للاتباع الفعلي الثابت في حجة الوداع على ما رواه مسلم من أنه عليه السلام نحر ثلاثاً وستين بدنة بيده ونحر علي ما بقي من المائة، ثم أمر من كل بدنة ببضعة فجعلت في قدر فطبخت من لحمها وشربا من مرقها، ولأنه دم النسك فيجوز منه الأكل كالأضحية. وأشار بكلمة «من» إلى أنه يأكل البعض منه والمستحب أن يفعل كما في الأضحية وهو أن يتصدق بالثلث ويطعم الأغنياء الثلث ويأكل ويدخر الثلث. وأفاد بقوله «هدي التطوع» أنه بلغ الحرم، أما إذا ذبحه قبل بلوغه فليس بهدي فلم يدخل تحت عبارته ليحتاج إلى الاستثناء فلهذا لا يأكل منه والفرق بينهما إنه إذا بلغ الحرم فالقربة فيه بالإراقة وقد حصلت والأكل بعد
His statement: 'And he eats from voluntary hadi, and tamattu', and qiran only' means he is permitted to eat and it is recommended due to the practical following established in the Farewell Pilgrimage as reported by Muslim that he (peace be upon him) slaughtered sixty-three camels with his own hand, and Ali slaughtered the remainder of the hundred, then he ordered that from each camel a piece be taken, placed in a pot, cooked from its meat, and they drank from its broth. And because it is the blood of the ritual, so eating from it is permissible like udhiya. And he indicated with the word 'from' that he eats some of it, and the recommended is to act as in udhiya: to give one third in charity, feed the rich one third, and eat and store one third. And he benefitted by his statement 'voluntary hadi' that it has reached the Haram. As for if he slaughters it before it reaches, it is not hadi, so it does not fall under his expression to need an exception; thus he does not eat from it. The difference between them is that if it reaches the Haram, the worship lies in the shedding of blood, and it has occurred, and eating after
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about eating from the Hadi. The author states that the person offering Hadi may eat from voluntary (nafl) Hadi, from the tamattu' sacrifice, and from the qiran sacrifice. This is recommended based on the Prophet's action in Hajjat al-Wada' where he ate from the meat. The proof is a hadith from Muslim. The rule is analogized to Udhiya (Eid sacrifice) where eating is also permissible. The recommended distribution is to split the meat into three parts: charity, gifting to rich, and keeping for oneself. Importantly, this only applies if the animal has reached the Haram (i.e., the sacred precincts); if slaughtered before reaching the Haram, it is not considered Hadi and the rules do not apply. The commentator also clarifies that the word 'from' indicates eating only part, not all.

Key Terms
هدي التطوع (hady al-tatawwu'): Voluntary Hadi, not obligatory due to Hajj rites.
دم المتعة (dam al-tamattu'): The sacrifice required for performing Tamattu' Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) From voluntary, tamattu', and qiran Hadi
C) From all types of Hadi including penalties
D) From no Hadi; it must all be given in charity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows and recommends eating from voluntary, tamattu', and qiran Hadi, but not from obligatory penalties.

Exceptions to Sheep in Hadi: Janabah and Intercourse after Wuquf
Explanation of the two exceptions to the general rule of sheep in Hadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions to the general rule that a sheep suffices for all Hadi, explain the reason for requiring a camel in those cases, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding intercourse after halq.

Core Idea
Sheep suffices for all Hadi except tawaf al-rukn while junub and intercourse after wuquf—where only a camel suffices.

You already know that a sheep (shah) is sufficient for most cases requiring a blood sacrifice (dam) in Hajj. But did you know there are two exceptions where only a camel (badanah) will do? This lesson unpacks those exceptions and the reasoning behind them, as explained in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. We'll also clarify a subtle dispute among Hanafi scholars about the timing of the intercourse exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the author's statement that the sheep is permissible in every place where blood (dam) is mentioned in the Book of Hajj.
The general rule applies to all mandatory expiations in Hajj, such as the dam for leaving a wajib or committing a prohibited act.
2
Spot the two exceptions
Identify the two specific cases where a sheep does not suffice: tawaf al-rukn performed while junub and intercourse after wuquf (before halq and tawaf).
Tawaf al-rukn is the obligatory tawaf of Hajj (tawaf al-ziyarah). Junub is a state of major ritual impurity.
3
Understand the ‘illah (reason) for the camel requirement
Analyze the commentator's reasoning: janabah is 'more severe' (aghlaz) so a camel is required to show the difference between minor and major impurities. For intercourse after wuquf, it is the 'highest type of intimacy' so the obligation is also elevated.
The same reasoning applies to a woman in menstruation or postpartum bleeding, as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Resolve the intra-Hanafi dispute about the timing of intercourse
The passage notes that some scholars apply the camel rule even to intercourse after halq, but the preponderant (rajih) view is that it applies only to intercourse after wuquf but before halq and tawaf.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi (operative position) is the preponderant view.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Test your understanding: A pilgrim performs tawaf al-rukn while in a state of menstruation. Does a sheep suffice? Why or why not?
The reasoning links menstruation to janabah in severity, so the same ruling applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب الهدي في كل شيء إلا في طواف الركن جنباً ووطء بعد الوقوف ويأكل من هدى التطوع ولا يجوز في الهدايا إلا ما جاز في الضحايا فإنه لا يلزم من الاطراد الانعكاس، ألا ترى إلى قولهم وما جاز أن يكون ثمناً في البيع جاز أن يكون أجرة في الإجارة لم يلزم انعكاسه لفساده لجواز جعل المنافع المختلفة أجرة لا ثمناً قوله : (والشاة تجوز في كل شيء إلا في طواف الركن جنباً ووطء بعد الوقوف) يعني أن كل موضع ذكر فيه الدم من كتاب الحج تجزئ فيه الشاة إلا فيما ذكره، وليس مراده التعميم فإن من نذر بدنة أو جزوراً لا تجزئه الشاة وإنما لزمت البدنة فيما إذا طاف جنباً لأن الجنابة أغلظ فيجب جبر نقصانها بالبدنة إظهاراً للتفاوت بين الأصغر والأكبر. ويلحق به ما إذا طافت حائضاً أو نفساء وليس موضعاً ثالثاً كما في فتح القدير، لأن المعنى الموجب للتغليظ واحد ووجبت في الجماع بعد الوقوف لأنه أعلى أنواع الارتفاقات فيتغلظ موجبه . وأطلق فشمل ما بعد الحلق وقد أسلفنا فيه اختلافاً، الشاة والراجح وجوب بعده، فالمراد هنا الوطء بعد الوقوف قبل الحلق والطواف .
The Book of Hajj, Chapter of Hadi: It is permissible in everything except in tawaf al-rukn (the obligatory tawaf) in a state of janabah and intercourse after wuquf, and he eats from the Hadi of tatawwu'. And it is not permissible in Hadi except what is permissible in Udhiyah, for the rule that what establishes the converse is not necessary. Do you not see their saying: 'What is permissible as a price in sale is permissible as a wage in hire'? Its converse does not necessarily hold because of its invalidity, since different benefits are permissible as wages but not as price. He said: (And the sheep is permissible in everything except in tawaf al-rukn while junub and intercourse after wuquf) meaning that every place where blood (dam) is mentioned in the Book of Hajj, a sheep suffices for it except what he mentioned. But he does not intend generality, for if one vows a camel (badanah) or a young camel (jazur), a sheep does not suffice. Rather, the badanah is required when he performs tawaf while junub because janabah is more severe, so its deficiency must be compensated with a badanah to manifest the difference between the minor and major. Included with it is when a woman performs tawaf while in menstruation or postpartum bleeding, and there is no third place as in Fath al-Qadir, because the meaning requiring severity is one. And it (badanah) is required for intercourse after wuquf because it is the highest type of intimacy, so its cause becomes severe. And it is absolute, so it includes after halq, and we have previously mentioned there is a difference of opinion, the sheep (is also valid) but the preponderant view is that it is obligatory after it. So here the intended meaning is intercourse after wuquf before halq and tawaf.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the exception to the general rule that a sheep is sufficient for all blood sacrifices (dam) in Hajj. The commentator explains that the matn's statement 'in everything' is general but not absolute; wherever a sacrifice is prescribed, a sheep suffices, except for two cases: tawaf al-rukn (the obligatory tawaf of Hajj, i.e., tawaf al-ziyarah) performed in a state of janabah (major ritual impurity requiring ghusl), and sexual intercourse after wuquf at Arafat. For these, a camel (badanah) is required due to the severity of the impurity or the act. The commentator also notes that this includes a woman in menstruation or postpartum bleeding (since the impurity is of similar gravity). The discussion then addresses the timing of the intercourse exception: some hold it applies even after halq (shaving), but the preferred view is that it applies only to intercourse after wuquf but before halq and tawaf. The initial part about the analogy with Udhiyah clarifies that the validity of an animal in Udhiyah does not necessarily imply validity in Hadi in all respects (the converse of the rule does not hold). This is a key Hanafi principle: the ruling is not reversible.

Key Terms
طواف الركن (tawaf al-rukn): The obligatory tawaf of Hajj (tawaf al-ziyarah) that is a pillar (rukn) of Hajj.
جنب (junub): A state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, such as after sexual intercourse or seminal emission.
بدنة (badanah): A camel or cow used as a sacrificial animal; in this context it refers specifically to a camel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require a camel (badanah) for tawaf al-rukn performed while in a state of janabah?
A) Because janabah is a greater impurity requiring a greater expiation to show the difference between minor and major.
B) Because sheep are not allowed for any tawaf at all.
C) Because the Prophet (saws) did so only once.
D) Because it is a punishment for the sin.

Key Takeaway
A sheep suffices for all Hadi except tawaf al-rukn while junub and intercourse after wuquf before halq and tawaf—both require a camel due to the severity of the cause.

Eating from Hadi: Permissibility and Conditions (Tatawwu', Tamattu', Qiran)
Explanation of ruling on eating from Hadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating from different types of Hadi and identify the condition that the animal must have reached the Haram.

Core Idea
Permissible to eat from voluntary, tamattu', and qiran Hadi only — the animal must reach the Haram.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the ruling on eating from the meat of sacrificial animals (Hadi) brought during Hajj. It establishes permissibility for three specific types: voluntary Hadi (tatawwu'), the Hadi of tamattu', and the Hadi of qiran. The proof is drawn from the Prophet's own practice during the Farewell Pilgrimage and analogy with the Eid sacrifice (udhiyah). A key condition is that the animal must have entered the sacred precinct (Haram) before slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of Hadi
List the three types of Hadi from which eating is permissible: voluntary (tatawwu'), tamattu', and qiran. Note that other types (e.g., Hadi of ifrad) are not included.
The matn restricts eating to these three categories only.
2
Recognize the dalil (proof)
The proof is twofold: (1) The Prophet's fi'l (action) during the Farewell Pilgrimage – he slaughtered and then ate and drank from the broth. (2) Qiyas with udhiyah (Eid sacrifice), since both are dam al-nusuk.
The Hanafi school uses fi'l as a dalil for recommendation (sunnah muakkadah).
3
Understand the condition
The Hadi must have reached the Haram before slaughter. If slaughtered on the way, it is not considered Hadi and one may not eat from it.
The distinction: if it reaches the Haram, the act of worship (qurbah) is accomplished by pouring blood, and eating is permitted afterwards.
4
Note the recommended division
The recommended practice is to divide the meat into three thirds: one third given in charity, one third gifted to the rich (as a gift), and one third kept for oneself and family.
This is analogous to udhiyah.
5
Distinguish from excluded types
Obligatory Hadi (e.g., dam al-jana'ib) is not included in this permission. Those must be given entirely in charity.
The passage focuses only on voluntary, tamattu', and qiran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأكلا قوله : ( ويأكل من هدى التطوع والمتعة والقرآن فقط) أي يجوز له الأكل ويستحب للاتباع الفعلي الثابت في حجة الوداع على ما رواه مسلم من أنه عليه السلام نحر ثلاثاً وستين بدنة بيده ونحر علي ما بقي من المائة، ثم أمر من كل بدنة ببضعة فجعلت في قدر فطبخت من لحمها وشربا من مرقها، ولأنه دم النسك فيجوز منه الأكل كالأضحية. وأشار بكلمة «من» إلى أنه يأكل البعض منه والمستحب أن يفعل كما في الأضحية وهو أن يتصدق بالثلث ويطعم الأغنياء الثلث ويأكل ويدخر الثلث. وأفاد بقوله «هدي التطوع» أنه بلغ الحرم، أما إذا ذبحه قبل بلوغه فليس بهدي فلم يدخل تحت عبارته ليحتاج إلى الاستثناء فلهذا لا يأكل منه والفرق بينهما إنه إذا بلغ الحرم فالقربة فيه بالإراقة وقد حصلت والأكل بعد .
So they both ate. He said: (And he eats from the Hadi of tatawwu', mut'ah, and qiran only) meaning it is permissible for him to eat, and it is recommended due to the practical following established in the Farewell Pilgrimage as narrated by Muslim: that the Prophet (saws) slaughtered sixty-three camels with his own hand, and Ali slaughtered the remaining of the hundred, then he ordered a piece from each camel to be placed in a pot, so they cooked meat from it and drank from its broth. And because it is a sacrificial animal (dam al-nusuk), it is permissible to eat from it like the udhiyah. And he indicated with the word 'min' that he eats some of it, and the recommended is to do as in udhiyah: to give charity with a third, feed the rich a third, and eat and store a third. And he indicated by his statement 'Hadi of tatawwu'' that it has reached the Haram. But if he slaughters it before reaching the Haram, it is not Hadi, so it is not included under his statement that would require an exception, therefore he does not eat from it. The difference between them is that if it reaches the Haram, the act of worship is in the pouring of blood and it has been accomplished, and eating comes afterward.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on eating from the meat of Hadi. The commentator, quoting the matn, states that it is permissible to eat from voluntary Hadi (tatawwu'), the Hadi of tamattu' (mut'ah), and the Hadi of qiran. The permissibility is proven by the practice of the Prophet (saws) during the Farewell Pilgrimage, as narrated by Muslim: he slaughtered many camels and then ate and drank from their broth. Additionally, qiyas (analogy) with udhiyah (Eid sacrifice) supports this, since both are sacrificial animals (dam al-nusuk). The recommended way is to give a third in charity, a third as gifts to the rich, and to keep a third for oneself and family. A key condition is that the Hadi must have reached the Haram (the sacred precinct of Makkah) before slaughter; if it is slaughtered on the way, it is not considered Hadi and one may not eat from it.

Key Terms
هدي التطوع (hadi al-tatawwu'): A voluntary sacrificial animal brought by a pilgrim not as an obligatory part of Hajj but as a supererogatory act.
هدي المتعة والقرآن (hadi al-mut'ah wa al-qiran): The sacrificial animal required for the tamattu' and qiran types of Hajj (non-ifrad).
دم النسك (dam al-nusuk): Blood of sacrifice; refers to animals slaughtered as an act of worship (nusuk) in Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the recommended division of meat from a voluntary Hadi according to the Hanafi school?
A) Give all in charity.
B) Keep half, give half to relatives.
C) Give a third in charity, a third as gifts to the rich, and keep a third.
D) Eat only, no sharing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits eating from Hadi of tatawwu', tamattu', and qiran, provided the animal has reached the Haram; the recommended distribution is thirds.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Charity from Sold Hadi Meat
Intra-Hanafi dispute on charity from sold Hadi meat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of al-Bada'i and al-Fath regarding charity when selling Hadi meat and apply the preferred view.

Core Idea
Charity from sold Hadi meat depends on whether the poor have a pre-existing right in the meat.

This lesson examines a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about the sale of Hadi meat. While both al-Bada'i and al-Fath agree that selling is generally permissible, they differ on the requirement and method of charity when the meat is sold. We will parse the arguments, identify the points of contradiction, and see how later Hanafi authorities reconcile the two views.

Guided Path
1
Classify Hadi Types
Identify the two categories: Hadi where eating is permissible (e.g., voluntary that reached Haram, udhiyah) and Hadi where eating is not permissible (expiations, vows, ihsar).
This classification determines whether the poor have a right attached to the meat.
2
State al-Bada'i's Ruling
According to al-Bada'i, for permissible-eating Hadi, no charity is required upon sale; for non-permissible Hadi, charity with the sale price is required.
3
State al-Fath's Ruling
According to al-Fath, it is not permissible to sell any Hadi meat; if sold, charity must be given with the market value.
4
Analyze Points of Contradiction
Identify two contradictions: (1) whether charity is required for permissible-eating Hadi, and (2) whether charity is given with price or value for non-permissible Hadi.
5
Apply the Preferred View
Use the reconciliation: for non-permissible eating Hadi, give charity with value (to fulfill the poor's right), but if the price is higher, some opt for the price. For permissible-eating Hadi, charity is not required from the meat itself, but if sold, charity is given with value to avoid difference.

And there remains a contradiction from another aspect, that the apparent meaning of al-Bada'i is no obligation to give charity with anything in what he may eat, because he specifies obligation for what he may not eat, while the apparent words of al-Fath require charity in both. Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah-style) discussing the rules of selling Hadi meat and the obligation to give charity. The text begins by reiterating that eating is not permissible from other types of Hadi (expiations, vows, ihsar). Then it quotes al-Bada'i: for Hadi where eating is permissible, one is not required to give charity with the meat; for Hadi where eating is not permissible, one must give charity. If the animal perishes after slaughter, no liability. If one consumes it improperly, guarantee of value. Selling is permissible, but if the meat was from a type where eating is prohibited, one must give charity with the price. The passage then compares this with the statement in al-Fath al-Qadir, which says that one should not sell any Hadi meat, and if sold, one must give charity with the value. The commentator (likely from al-Nahr) notes a contradiction between al-Bada'i and al-Fath on two points: (1) whether charity is required in the case where eating is permissible, and (2) whether one should consider the price or the value when charity is due. Various reconciliation attempts are discussed, including considering the price if higher than the value. The passage ends by preferring the view of al-Fath (charity with value) for the case where eating is not permissible, and al-Bada'i for the permissible case, with the clarification that 'permissible' in al-Fath refers to validity of sale, not unrestricted permissibility. The overall result is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute resolution.

Key Terms
دم الكفارات (dam al-kafarat): Blood of expiations: sacrifices required as penalty for certain violations during Hajj.
هدي الإحصار (hadi al-ihsar): Sacrificial animal due for being prevented from completing Hajj.
التصدق (al-tasadduq): Giving in charity; in this context, distributing meat or its monetary value to the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must give charity with the price.
B) He must give charity with the value.
C) He must keep the money without charity.
D) He must return the sale.

Key Takeaway
When selling Hadi meat, charity is required only for types where eating is prohibited, and the amount should be the market value according to the preferred view.

Eating from the Hadi and Charity Obligations: An Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Explains permissibility of eating from different Hadi types and charity duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the categories of hadi where eating is permissible and prohibited, and apply the Hanafi ruling on charity when the meat is sold or consumed, reconciling the views of al-Badai' and Fath al-Qadir.

Core Idea
Eating from hadi is permissible only for Tamattu' and Qiran; selling or consuming other types requires charity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles two intertwined rulings: which sacrificial animals (hadi) may the pilgrim eat from, and what charity obligations arise if the meat is sold or consumed. The author navigates a subtle internal Hanafi dispute between al-Badai' and Fath al-Qadir over whether charity is due on the price or the value when the meat is sold. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct application in Hajj rituals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of hadi
List the types of hadi mentioned and determine whether eating from each is permissible or prohibited according to the opening statement.
Categories: Tamattu', Qiran, Kaffarah, Nadhr, Ihsar, Voluntary not reaching Haram.
2
Understand the basis for the ruling
Explain why each category is treated differently: voluntary vs expiatory, attachment of poor's right.
Expiation bloods are charity by nature; consumption nullifies the poor's right.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the positions of al-Badai' (no charity for permissible category) and Fath al-Qadir (charity with value if sold). Identify the point of conflict.
The conflict is whether selling meat from permissible category requires charity at all.
4
Apply the reconciliation
Use the author's reconciliation to determine the mufta bihi: permissible category → charity with value; prohibited category → charity with price.
'Permissible' in al-Badai' means validity (sihhah) not lawful (halal).
5
Extend to guarantee upon consumption
Conclude the ruling on guarantee when the owner eats prohibited hadi: only the consumed portion is guaranteed (Hanafi) vs entire animal (Maliki).
Hanafi view restricts guarantee to amount consumed; Maliki view is full guarantee.

And it appears that the intended meaning of consideration is what he presented earlier, and you are aware that there is no reason to mention the first respect, because the obligation of charity with the value of what is eaten does not necessitate the obligation of charity with itself, like the udhiya: charity with it is not obligatory, and if he sells its skin or some of its meat for a consumable or dirhams, charity with the price is obligatory, so it is not contradictory to the statement of al-Badai', 'charity with its meat is not obligatory.' And by what we mentioned you know the rejection falls away, for the udhiya is his property and consideration is given to the price in it, so in our issue consideration is given to the value, otherwise what is the difference between them? In summary, the contradiction is apparent in the second respect, which is the obligation of charity in what is not permissible for him to eat with the price according to al-Badai', and with the value according to al-Fath, and a contradiction remains from another respect, which is that the apparent meaning of al-Badai' is the non-obligation of charity with anything in what is permissible for him to eat, because it specifies the obligation of charity in what is not permissible, and the apparent meaning of the statement of al-Fath is the obligation of charity in both. The reconciliation that the author mentioned is that one qualifies his statement between them: if he sells from what is not permissible to eat, charity with the price is obligatory and the value is not considered; if he sells from what is not permissible for him to eat (sic, but likely should say 'permissible'), charity with the value is obligatory and the price is not considered; and that the intended meaning of permissibility in the statement of al-Badai' is validity not legality. And in Fath al-Qadir: If he eats from what is not permissible for him to eat from it, he guarantees what he ate, and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said the same, and Malik said: If he ate a mouthful, he guarantees all of it. His saying: (And if he sells from what is not permissible for him to eat) This is how it is found in many copies with the negative 'la' here and in the preceding, and the correct is to delete it here as found in some copies. End. And it is in agreement with the apparent meaning of the statement of al-Badai'.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, chapter of Hadi. It addresses the ruling on eating from sacrificial animals (hadi) and the resulting charity obligations. The author begins by stating that the permissibility of eating from the hadi is restricted: one may eat only from the hadi of Tamattu' and Qiran, not from other types such as expiation bloods (dima' al-kaffarat), vowed offerings (nudhur), the hadi of ihsar (restriction), or voluntary animals that did not reach the Haram. Rich individuals are also prohibited from eating. The reasoning is that these categories are charity (sadaqah) or expiation, and consumption would nullify the rights of the poor. The passage then quotes Imam al-Kasani's al-Badai' al-Sana'i', which distinguishes between two categories: (1) hadi from which eating is permissible does not require charity with the meat after slaughter; (2) hadi from which eating is prohibited requires full charity with the meat. If the animal is lost after slaughter, no liability arises. If the owner consumes the meat himself, he must guarantee its value if it was from the prohibited category (since the poor's right attached), but not if from the permissible category. Selling the meat is valid in both cases because ownership remains, but in the prohibited category, the sale price must be given in charity. The commentator then notes a conflict between al-Badai' and the transmission in Fath al-Qadir, which states that selling is not allowed at all, and if done, the value must be given in charity. A lengthy reconciliation follows, attempting to harmonize the texts. The preferred view is that in the prohibited category, charity is with the price (thaman), and in the permissible category, charity is with the value (qimah) if sold, because the owner had permission to eat but chose to sell. The discussion includes responses to objections and clarification that 'permissibility' in al-Badai' means validity (sihhah) not lawful (halal). The passage concludes with a textual note about a variant reading in the manuscript.

Key Terms
هدي (hadi): Sacrificial animal brought to the Haram for Hajj or Umrah; specifically refers to the animal offered in the Haram.
دم (dam): Blood; in this context, the animal sacrificed as expiation or offering.
التصدق (al-tasaddaq): Giving in charity the meat or its value to the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling the meat of a hadi that is prohibited to eat (e.g., kaffarah)?
A) The sale is invalid and the meat must be given away
B) The sale is valid but the price must be given in charity
C) The sale is valid and the seller may keep the price
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer is poor

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of eating from hadi determines the charity obligation: prohibited categories require charity with the price if sold; permissible categories require charity with the value if sold.
